## Chapter 18: Design for Manufacturability




### Introduction

In the world of engineering, cost is a crucial factor that cannot be overlooked. It is a determining factor in the success of a product or project. As such, it is essential for engineers to consider cost effectiveness in their design process. This chapter will delve into the concept of design for cost effectiveness, providing a comprehensive guide for engineers to understand and implement this crucial aspect of engineering design.

Design for cost effectiveness is a systematic approach to designing products or systems that balances the need for functionality with the need for cost efficiency. It involves considering the cost implications of design decisions at every stage of the design process, from concept generation to final design. This approach is crucial in today's competitive market, where products must not only be functional but also cost-effective.

This chapter will cover various topics related to design for cost effectiveness, including cost estimation, cost reduction strategies, and cost-benefit analysis. It will also discuss the role of rapid prototyping in cost-effective design, and how it can be used to test and refine designs before they are mass-produced.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of design for cost effectiveness and its importance in engineering. They will also have the tools and knowledge to apply these concepts in their own design processes, leading to more cost-effective and successful products.




### Section: 18.1 Cost Considerations in Design:

In the previous chapter, we discussed the concept of design-to-cost (DTC) and its importance in product development. In this section, we will delve deeper into the role of cost in design decisions.

#### 18.1a Understanding the role of cost in design decisions

Cost is a critical factor in design decisions. It is not just about minimizing expenses, but also about maximizing value. As mentioned in the previous chapter, DTC is about considering cost as a design parameter in your product development activities. This means that cost is not just a constraint, but a design objective that needs to be balanced with other objectives such as functionality and schedule.

The role of cost in design decisions can be understood in terms of the project triangle, which consists of three parameters: feature scope, schedule, and cost. These three parameters are interrelated, and changing one can affect the others. For example, reducing the cost of a product may require a reduction in its feature scope or an extension of its development schedule.

To make informed design decisions, engineers need to consider the cost implications of their decisions at every stage of the design process. This requires a deep understanding of the product, its components, and the manufacturing process. It also requires a systematic approach to cost management, which includes techniques such as target costing and design-to-cost.

In the next section, we will discuss these cost management techniques in more detail and provide practical examples of how they can be applied in engineering design.

#### 18.1b Techniques for cost estimation in design

Cost estimation is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It involves predicting the cost of a product or system based on its design and specifications. This is crucial in the early stages of product development, where decisions can have a significant impact on the overall cost of the product.

There are several techniques for cost estimation in design, each with its own strengths and limitations. These include:

1. **Bottom-up cost estimation:** This is a detailed approach that involves breaking down the product into its components and estimating the cost of each component. This can be time-consuming but provides a more accurate estimate.

2. **Top-down cost estimation:** This is a more simplified approach that involves estimating the cost of the product based on its overall size or complexity. This is often used in the early stages of product development when detailed design information is not yet available.

3. **Parametric cost estimation:** This approach uses mathematical models to estimate the cost of a product based on its design parameters. This can be a powerful tool for cost estimation, but it requires a good understanding of the product and its manufacturing process.

4. **Cost database:** This involves using historical cost data to estimate the cost of a product. This can be a useful tool for cost estimation, but it requires a large and relevant database of cost data.

5. **Cost simulation:** This involves using computer simulations to estimate the cost of a product. This can be a powerful tool for cost estimation, but it requires a good understanding of the product and its manufacturing process, as well as access to relevant cost data.

In the next section, we will discuss these cost estimation techniques in more detail and provide practical examples of how they can be applied in engineering design.

#### 18.1c Case studies of cost effective design

In this section, we will explore some real-world examples of cost effective design. These case studies will provide practical insights into how the concepts and techniques discussed in the previous sections are applied in engineering design.

##### Case Study 1: Design-to-Cost at Tesla

Tesla, a leading electric vehicle manufacturer, is known for its innovative design and cost-effective production. The company's design-to-cost approach is a key factor in its success. Tesla's design team is tasked with creating a product that meets the company's cost objectives, which are set based on market and competitive considerations. This approach allows Tesla to deliver high-quality products at competitive prices.

Tesla's design-to-cost approach involves a systematic process that includes cost estimation, cost reduction, and cost monitoring. The company uses a combination of bottom-up and top-down cost estimation techniques to predict the cost of its products. It also employs a range of cost reduction strategies, including value engineering, target costing, and design-to-cost.

##### Case Study 2: Cost Estimation at Boeing

Boeing, a leading aircraft manufacturer, uses a variety of cost estimation techniques in its design process. The company's cost estimation process includes bottom-up cost estimation, top-down cost estimation, and parametric cost estimation.

Boeing's bottom-up cost estimation involves breaking down the aircraft into its components and estimating the cost of each component. This approach is particularly useful in the early stages of product development, where detailed design information is not yet available.

Boeing's top-down cost estimation involves estimating the cost of the aircraft based on its overall size or complexity. This approach is often used in the later stages of product development, where more detailed design information is available.

Boeing's parametric cost estimation uses mathematical models to estimate the cost of the aircraft based on its design parameters. This approach is particularly useful for predicting the cost of complex systems, such as aircraft.

##### Case Study 3: Cost Reduction at Apple

Apple, a leading technology company, is known for its innovative design and cost-effective production. The company's cost reduction strategies include value engineering, target costing, and design-to-cost.

Apple's value engineering process involves identifying and eliminating unnecessary costs in the product design. This can include simplifying the design, reducing the number of components, or sourcing components from lower-cost suppliers.

Apple's target costing approach involves setting a target cost for the product and then working backwards to design a product that meets this cost target. This approach requires a deep understanding of the product and its manufacturing process, as well as a good understanding of market and competitive considerations.

Apple's design-to-cost approach involves designing the product with cost in mind. This can include considering the cost implications of design decisions at every stage of the design process, as well as incorporating cost reduction strategies into the design process.

In the next section, we will discuss these cost estimation and reduction techniques in more detail and provide practical examples of how they can be applied in engineering design.




#### 18.1b Techniques for cost estimation in design

Cost estimation is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It involves predicting the cost of a product or system based on its design and specifications. This is crucial in the early stages of product development, where decisions can have a significant impact on the overall cost of the product.

There are several techniques for cost estimation in design, each with its own strengths and limitations. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: top-down and bottom-up.

##### Top-Down Cost Estimation

Top-down cost estimation is a method that starts with a high-level estimate of the total cost of the product or system, and then breaks it down into smaller components. This method is often used in the early stages of product development, where the design is not fully defined and the cost is estimated based on similar products or systems.

The top-down cost estimation can be expressed mathematically as follows:

$$
C_{total} = C_{1} + C_{2} + ... + C_{n}
$$

where $C_{total}$ is the total cost, and $C_{i}$ are the individual costs of the components.

##### Bottom-Up Cost Estimation

Bottom-up cost estimation, on the other hand, starts with the individual costs of the components and sums them up to get the total cost. This method is often used in later stages of product development, where the design is more detailed and the cost can be estimated more accurately.

The bottom-up cost estimation can be expressed mathematically as follows:

$$
C_{total} = C_{1} + C_{2} + ... + C_{n}
$$

where $C_{total}$ is the total cost, and $C_{i}$ are the individual costs of the components.

Both top-down and bottom-up cost estimation techniques have their own advantages and disadvantages. Top-down estimation is faster and easier to perform, but it may not be as accurate as bottom-up estimation. Bottom-up estimation, on the other hand, is more accurate, but it requires more detailed design information and can be time-consuming.

In the next section, we will discuss some practical examples of how these cost estimation techniques can be applied in engineering design.

#### 18.1c Case studies in cost effectiveness

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that illustrate the application of cost effectiveness in engineering design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be applied to solve real-world problems.

##### Case Study 1: Cost Effectiveness in the Design of a Smart Home System

The first case study involves the design of a smart home system. The system is designed to automate various tasks in a home, such as lighting control, temperature control, and security. The goal is to design a system that is cost-effective, reliable, and easy to use.

The top-down cost estimation method is used in this case. The total cost of the system is estimated based on the cost of similar systems in the market. The individual costs of the components, such as sensors, actuators, and control units, are then estimated based on their functionality and the cost of similar components.

The bottom-up cost estimation method is also used in this case. The individual costs of the components are summed up to get the total cost of the system. This method allows for a more detailed and accurate cost estimation, but it also requires more detailed design information.

##### Case Study 2: Cost Effectiveness in the Design of a Medical Device

The second case study involves the design of a medical device used for patient monitoring. The device is designed to monitor vital signs such as heart rate, blood pressure, and oxygen levels. The goal is to design a device that is cost-effective, accurate, and easy to use.

The top-down cost estimation method is used in this case. The total cost of the device is estimated based on the cost of similar devices in the market. The individual costs of the components, such as sensors, microcontrollers, and power supply, are then estimated based on their functionality and the cost of similar components.

The bottom-up cost estimation method is also used in this case. The individual costs of the components are summed up to get the total cost of the device. This method allows for a more detailed and accurate cost estimation, but it also requires more detailed design information.

These case studies illustrate the importance of cost effectiveness in engineering design. By using techniques such as top-down and bottom-up cost estimation, engineers can design products that are cost-effective, reliable, and easy to use.




#### 18.1c Case studies in design for cost effectiveness

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of cost considerations in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be applied in different industries and contexts.

##### Case Study 1: Energy Efficient Buildings

The Energy Retrofit Loan Analysis Model is a case study that demonstrates the application of target costing in the energy sector. This model, developed using a Monte Carlo method, is used to determine the impact of financial uncertainties on the performance of Energy Efficient buildings. The model has been shown to be effective in evaluating the costs and uncertainties involved in the energy efficiency process.

The Target Value Design Decision Making Process (TVD-DMP) is another example of target costing in the energy sector. This process groups a set of energy efficiency methods at different optimization levels to evaluate costs and uncertainties involved in the energy efficiency process. Some major design parameters are specified using this methods including Facility Operation Schedule, Orientation, Plug load, HVAC and lighting systems.

The entire process consists of three phases: initiation, definition and alignment. The initiation stage involves developing a business case for energy efficiency using target value design (TVD) training, organization and compensation. The definition process involves defining and validating the case by tools such as values analysis and bench marking processes to determine the allowable costs. By setting targets and designing the design process to align with those targets, TVD-DMP has been shown to achieve a high level of collaboration needed for energy efficiency investments. This is done by using risk analysis tools, pull planning and rapid estimating processes.

##### Case Study 2: Healthcare Facilities

Target costing and target value design have applications in building healthcare facilities including critical components such as Neonatal Intensive Care Units (NICUs). The process is influenced by unit locations, degree of comfort, number of patients per room, type of supply location and access to nature. According to National Vital Statistics Reports, 12.18% of 2009 births were premature and the cost per infant was $51,000.

These case studies provide valuable insights into the practical application of cost considerations in design. They demonstrate how target costing and target value design can be used to optimize the design process and achieve cost effectiveness in different industries and contexts.




#### 18.2a Understanding value engineering

Value engineering (VE) is a systematic approach to designing products or systems that provides the necessary functions at the lowest cost possible. It is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness, as it helps to ensure that the final product or system is not only functional but also cost-efficient.

The concept of value engineering is closely tied to the principles of value-based engineering (VBE), which is a methodology that integrates ethical considerations into the design process. VBE complements the IEEE St. 7000 standard by introducing ten principles essential for addressing ethical concerns during system design. These principles are:

1. Ethical considerations should be integrated into the design process from the beginning.
2. The design should consider the impact on society, the environment, and future generations.
3. The design should be inclusive and consider the needs and perspectives of all stakeholders.
4. The design should be transparent and accountable, with clear decision-making processes.
5. The design should be resilient and able to adapt to changing circumstances.
6. The design should be sustainable and have a positive impact on the environment.
7. The design should be equitable and fair, ensuring equal access and opportunities for all.
8. The design should be responsible and accountable for its impact on society and the environment.
9. The design should be ethical and align with moral principles and values.
10. The design should be ethical and align with legal and regulatory requirements.

These principles provide a framework for ethical decision-making in the design process, helping to ensure that the final product or system is not only cost-effective but also socially, environmentally, and ethically responsible.

However, there have been criticisms of VBE, particularly in terms of its practical application. Some argue that there are only a limited number of case studies that show that VBE is delivering on its promise to facilitate the development of innovative or even ethical systems. Others argue that the focus on ethical considerations can lead to increased costs and complexity, potentially undermining the goal of cost effectiveness.

Despite these criticisms, value engineering remains a crucial aspect of design for cost effectiveness. By integrating ethical considerations into the design process, engineers can ensure that their products or systems are not only cost-efficient but also socially, environmentally, and ethically responsible.

#### 18.2b Techniques for value engineering

Value engineering is a systematic approach that involves a series of techniques to identify and eliminate unnecessary costs in the design process. These techniques are designed to help engineers and designers make informed decisions that balance cost, performance, and functionality.

One of the key techniques used in value engineering is the Pugh matrix, a decision-making tool that helps to compare different design options based on their cost and performance. The Pugh matrix is a simple yet powerful tool that can be used to make decisions at various stages of the design process, from the initial concept design to the final detailed design.

The Pugh matrix is a two-dimensional matrix that plots the cost of each design option against its performance. The options are then ranked based on their cost and performance, with the lowest cost and highest performance options being ranked highest. This allows engineers and designers to make decisions based on a clear understanding of the trade-offs between cost and performance.

Another important technique in value engineering is the value analysis, a systematic approach to identifying and eliminating unnecessary costs in the design process. Value analysis involves a detailed examination of the design to identify components or features that are not essential to the performance of the system. These components or features can then be eliminated or simplified to reduce the cost of the system.

Value analysis can be particularly useful in the design of complex systems, where there may be many components or features that are not essential to the performance of the system. By systematically examining each component or feature, engineers and designers can identify and eliminate unnecessary costs, leading to a more cost-effective design.

In addition to these techniques, value engineering also involves a range of other methods and tools, including value mapping, value tree analysis, and value stream mapping. These methods and tools are designed to help engineers and designers identify and eliminate unnecessary costs in the design process, leading to a more cost-effective design.

However, it's important to note that value engineering is not just about cost reduction. It's also about ensuring that the design is effective and efficient, meeting the needs and requirements of the user while also being cost-effective. This requires a balance between cost, performance, and functionality, which can be achieved through the systematic application of value engineering techniques.

#### 18.2c Case studies in value engineering

Value engineering is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It involves a systematic approach to identifying and eliminating unnecessary costs in the design process. In this section, we will explore some case studies that demonstrate the application of value engineering in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: Value Engineering in the Design of a Smart Home System

In this case study, a team of engineers was tasked with designing a smart home system that could control various aspects of a home, such as lighting, heating, and security. The team used value engineering techniques to design a system that was both cost-effective and efficient.

The team began by creating a Pugh matrix to compare different design options based on their cost and performance. They then used value analysis to identify and eliminate unnecessary components or features in the system. This led to a simplified design that was both cost-effective and efficient.

The team also used value mapping to identify the key functions of the system and their associated costs. This helped them to focus on the most critical functions and eliminate unnecessary ones. They also used value tree analysis to identify the key components of the system and their associated costs. This helped them to identify components that could be simplified or eliminated to reduce the cost of the system.

Finally, the team used value stream mapping to identify and eliminate waste in the design process. This helped them to streamline the design process and reduce the overall cost of the system.

##### Case Study 2: Value Engineering in the Design of a Medical Device

In this case study, a team of engineers was tasked with designing a medical device that could be used to monitor a patient's vital signs. The team used value engineering techniques to design a device that was both cost-effective and efficient.

The team began by creating a Pugh matrix to compare different design options based on their cost and performance. They then used value analysis to identify and eliminate unnecessary components or features in the device. This led to a simplified design that was both cost-effective and efficient.

The team also used value mapping to identify the key functions of the device and their associated costs. This helped them to focus on the most critical functions and eliminate unnecessary ones. They also used value tree analysis to identify the key components of the device and their associated costs. This helped them to identify components that could be simplified or eliminated to reduce the cost of the device.

Finally, the team used value stream mapping to identify and eliminate waste in the design process. This helped them to streamline the design process and reduce the overall cost of the device.

These case studies demonstrate the power of value engineering in the design process. By systematically applying value engineering techniques, engineers and designers can create cost-effective and efficient designs that meet the needs and requirements of their users.




#### 18.2b Incorporating value engineering in design decisions

Value engineering (VE) is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It is a systematic approach to designing products or systems that provides the necessary functions at the lowest cost possible. Incorporating value engineering in design decisions is a crucial step towards achieving cost effectiveness in engineering design.

The process of value engineering involves a systematic analysis of the functions of various components and materials. This analysis is used to identify areas where costs can be reduced without compromising the performance or functionality of the product or system. This is achieved by manipulating the ratio of function to cost, which is defined as value.

The reasoning behind value engineering is that if a product is expected to become obsolete within a specific length of time, it can be designed to only last for that specific lifetime. This can be achieved by using higher-grade components, but this would impose an unnecessary cost on the manufacturer and to a limited extent also an increased cost on the purchaser. Value engineering, on the other hand, can reduce these costs.

However, due to the very short life spans, which is often a result of this "value engineering technique", planned obsolescence has become associated with product deterioration and inferior quality. This has led to criticism of value engineering, with some arguing that it gives engineering as a whole a bad name.

Despite these criticisms, value engineering remains a crucial aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It is a primary tenet of value engineering that basic functions be preserved and not be reduced as a consequence of pursuing value improvements. This is in line with the principles of value-based engineering (VBE), which integrates ethical considerations into the design process.

Incorporating value engineering in design decisions involves considering the ethical implications of the design. This includes considering the impact on society, the environment, and future generations. It also involves being inclusive and considering the needs and perspectives of all stakeholders. The design should be transparent and accountable, with clear decision-making processes. It should be resilient and able to adapt to changing circumstances. The design should be sustainable and have a positive impact on the environment. The design should be equitable and fair, ensuring equal access and opportunities for all. The design should be responsible and accountable for its impact on society and the environment. The design should be ethical and align with moral principles and values. Finally, the design should be ethical and align with legal and regulatory requirements.

In conclusion, incorporating value engineering in design decisions is a crucial step towards achieving cost effectiveness in engineering design. It involves a systematic analysis of the functions of various components and materials, and considering the ethical implications of the design. By doing so, engineers can design products or systems that provide the necessary functions at the lowest cost possible, while also ensuring that the design is socially, environmentally, and ethically responsible.

#### 18.2c Case studies in value engineering

Value engineering (VE) is a critical aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It is a systematic approach to designing products or systems that provides the necessary functions at the lowest cost possible. Incorporating value engineering in design decisions is a crucial step towards achieving cost effectiveness in engineering design.

To further illustrate the importance and effectiveness of value engineering, let's consider a case study in the automotive industry. The automotive industry is highly competitive, and manufacturers are constantly seeking ways to reduce costs while maintaining or improving product quality. This is where value engineering can be particularly beneficial.

Consider a car manufacturer that is designing a new model. The design team uses value engineering to systematically analyze the functions of various components and materials. They identify areas where costs can be reduced without compromising the performance or functionality of the car. For example, they may decide to use a less expensive but equally durable material for the car's body. This decision can significantly reduce the cost of production without compromising the car's performance or safety.

However, value engineering is not without its criticisms. Some argue that it leads to planned obsolescence, where products are designed to become obsolete after a short period of time. This is often due to the use of cheaper materials or components that may not last as long as higher-quality alternatives. However, value engineering can also be used to design products that are durable and long-lasting, providing value to both the manufacturer and the consumer.

In conclusion, value engineering is a crucial aspect of design for cost effectiveness. It allows engineers to systematically analyze the functions of various components and materials, identify areas for cost reduction, and design products that provide the necessary functions at the lowest cost possible. While it is important to consider the ethical implications of value engineering, when used responsibly, it can be a powerful tool for achieving cost effectiveness in engineering design.




#### 18.2c Case studies in value engineering

Value engineering is a practical approach to design that has been successfully applied in various industries. In this section, we will explore some case studies that demonstrate the application of value engineering in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: Reducing Costs in Automotive Design

In the automotive industry, value engineering has been used to reduce costs while maintaining performance and safety standards. For instance, in the design of a new car model, value engineering was used to optimize the use of materials and components. By analyzing the functions of each component and their cost, the design team was able to identify areas where costs could be reduced. This resulted in a significant reduction in the overall cost of the car without compromising its performance or safety.

##### Case Study 2: Improving Efficiency in Software Development

Value engineering has also been applied in the software industry. In the development of a new software application, value engineering was used to improve the efficiency of the development process. By analyzing the functions of each module and their cost, the development team was able to identify areas where costs could be reduced. This resulted in a more efficient development process, leading to cost savings and improved product quality.

##### Case Study 3: Enhancing Customer Satisfaction in Retail

In the retail industry, value engineering has been used to enhance customer satisfaction while reducing costs. For example, in the design of a new retail store, value engineering was used to optimize the layout and design of the store. By analyzing the functions of each design element and their cost, the design team was able to identify areas where costs could be reduced without compromising customer satisfaction. This resulted in a more cost-effective store design that enhanced customer satisfaction.

These case studies demonstrate the versatility and effectiveness of value engineering in various industries. By systematically analyzing the functions and costs of design elements, value engineering can lead to significant cost savings without compromising performance, safety, or customer satisfaction.

We have discussed the importance of considering cost factors in the design process and how it can impact the overall success of a project. We have also looked at various techniques and strategies that can be used to design for cost effectiveness, such as value engineering, cost-benefit analysis, and cost modeling.

One key takeaway from this chapter is the importance of understanding the relationship between cost and value. As engineers, it is crucial to not only focus on minimizing costs, but also on maximizing value. This requires a careful balance between cost and performance, and can be achieved through value engineering and cost-benefit analysis.

Another important aspect of design for cost effectiveness is the use of cost modeling. By creating a mathematical model that takes into account various cost factors, engineers can better understand the impact of design decisions on overall project costs. This allows for more informed decision-making and can lead to significant cost savings.

In conclusion, design for cost effectiveness is a crucial aspect of engineering design and rapid prototyping. By considering cost factors and utilizing various techniques and strategies, engineers can design more cost-effective and successful projects.

## Chapter: Engineering Design and Rapid Prototyping: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In today's fast-paced and competitive market, the ability to quickly and efficiently design and prototype new products is crucial for success. This is especially true in the field of engineering, where the demand for innovative and cost-effective solutions is constantly increasing. In this chapter, we will explore the concept of design for manufacturability, which is a crucial aspect of engineering design and rapid prototyping.

Design for manufacturability (DFM) is a design approach that focuses on creating products that are easy and cost-effective to manufacture. It involves considering the manufacturing process and its limitations during the design phase, rather than waiting until later stages of development. This approach not only saves time and resources, but also ensures that the final product is optimized for manufacturing.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to design for manufacturability, including design guidelines, design for assembly, and design for test. We will also discuss the importance of considering manufacturing constraints and limitations during the design process, and how to incorporate them into the design. Additionally, we will explore the role of rapid prototyping in the design for manufacturability process, and how it can be used to quickly test and refine designs.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of design for manufacturability and its importance in engineering design and rapid prototyping. They will also have the necessary knowledge and tools to apply these concepts in their own design processes, leading to more efficient and cost-effective products. So let's dive in and explore the world of design for manufacturability.

## Chapter 1:9: Design for Manufacturability:




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 19 of "Engineering Design and Rapid Prototyping: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will be discussing the crucial aspect of design for quality. As engineers, it is our responsibility to ensure that the products we design and prototype meet the highest standards of quality. This chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to understanding and implementing design for quality in your engineering projects.

Quality is a fundamental concept in engineering design and prototyping. It refers to the ability of a product to meet or exceed the expectations of its users. A product with high quality is not only functional but also reliable, durable, and safe to use. Designing for quality involves incorporating these qualities into the design process from the very beginning.

In this chapter, we will explore the various aspects of design for quality, including the principles and methodologies used, the role of rapid prototyping in quality assurance, and the challenges and solutions associated with designing for quality. We will also discuss real-world examples and case studies to provide a practical understanding of the concepts.

Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional engineer, this chapter will equip you with the knowledge and tools to design products that are of high quality and meet the needs and expectations of your users. So let's dive in and learn how to design for quality in engineering.




### Section: 19.1 Quality Considerations in Design:

Quality is a critical aspect of engineering design and rapid prototyping. It is the measure of how well a product or service meets the needs and expectations of its users. In this section, we will explore the various quality considerations that engineers must take into account during the design process.

#### 19.1a Understanding the role of quality in design decisions

Quality plays a pivotal role in design decisions. It is the foundation upon which all other design considerations are built. The quality of a product or service is determined by its ability to meet the needs and expectations of its users. Therefore, understanding the role of quality in design decisions is crucial for engineers.

Quality in design decisions is achieved when the design process is guided by a clear understanding of the product's purpose, perspective, and scope. This ensures that the right people are working on the right problem in the right way. It also helps to generate a variety of feasible and diverse options, which can be combined to create hybrid solutions that combine the best features of each option.

The quality of a design decision also depends on the quality of the information used to inform the decision. This information should be meaningful, reliable, and based on appropriate data and judgment. It should also reflect all uncertainties, biases, and assumptions.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the various quality considerations that engineers must take into account during the design process. We will also discuss how these considerations can be applied in practice to improve the quality of design decisions.

#### 19.1b Quality Assurance in Design

Quality assurance (QA) is a systematic process used to ensure that the quality requirements for a product or service will be fulfilled. It is a proactive approach that involves planning and executing processes to prevent mistakes or defects in products and services. Quality assurance is an essential part of the design process as it helps to identify and address potential quality issues early in the design phase, thereby reducing the likelihood of costly redesigns or recalls later in the product lifecycle.

The goal of quality assurance in design is to ensure that the product or service meets or exceeds the expectations of its users. This is achieved by applying a variety of tools and techniques to verify that the product or service conforms to the specified quality standards. These tools and techniques include design reviews, inspections, tests, and audits.

Design reviews, for instance, are a critical part of the quality assurance process. They involve a thorough examination of the design by a team of experts to identify and address potential quality issues. This can help to prevent costly redesigns or recalls later in the product lifecycle.

Inspections, on the other hand, are used to verify that the design conforms to the specified quality standards. They involve a detailed examination of the design to identify and address any discrepancies or non-conformities.

Tests are used to verify that the design meets the specified performance requirements. They involve subjecting the design to a series of tests to assess its performance under various conditions.

Audits are used to verify that the design process conforms to the specified quality standards. They involve a detailed examination of the design process to identify and address any non-conformities.

In conclusion, quality assurance plays a crucial role in the design process. It helps to ensure that the product or service meets or exceeds the expectations of its users, thereby enhancing customer satisfaction and loyalty. It also helps to reduce the likelihood of costly redesigns or recalls later in the product lifecycle.

#### 19.1c Case Studies of Quality in Design

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that highlight the importance of quality considerations in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how quality assurance is applied in the design process and the benefits it can bring.

##### Case Study 1: The Boeing 787 Dreamliner

The Boeing 787 Dreamliner is a mid-size widebody, mid-range jet airliner. It was designed to be more fuel-efficient and environmentally friendly than its predecessors. However, the design process was fraught with quality issues, leading to significant delays in the aircraft's introduction.

The quality issues were largely due to a lack of quality assurance in the design process. The design team failed to conduct thorough design reviews, inspections, and tests, which led to numerous design flaws and non-conformities. This resulted in costly redesigns and recalls, causing significant delays in the aircraft's introduction and increasing the overall cost of the project.

The case of the Boeing 787 Dreamliner underscores the importance of quality assurance in the design process. It highlights how a lack of quality assurance can lead to significant quality issues, delays, and cost overruns.

##### Case Study 2: The Apple iPhone

The Apple iPhone is a popular smartphone that has revolutionized the mobile phone industry. Its design process is a testament to the importance of quality assurance in design.

The design team at Apple applies a rigorous quality assurance process to ensure that the iPhone meets or exceeds the expectations of its users. This includes thorough design reviews, inspections, tests, and audits. The team also uses advanced design tools and techniques, such as computer-aided design (CAD) and finite element analysis (FEA), to verify the design's quality.

The quality assurance process at Apple has been instrumental in the iPhone's success. It has helped to ensure that the iPhone meets or exceeds the expectations of its users, thereby enhancing customer satisfaction and loyalty.

These case studies highlight the importance of quality considerations in design. They underscore the benefits of a systematic quality assurance process in the design process, including improved product quality, reduced costs, and faster time to market.




#### 19.1b Techniques for quality control in design

Quality control (QC) is a systematic process used to ensure that the quality requirements for a product or service will be met. It is a reactive approach that involves monitoring and inspecting products and services to identify and correct any defects or mistakes. 

There are several techniques that can be used for quality control in design. These include:

1. **Design Reviews:** Design reviews are a critical part of the design process. They involve a thorough examination of the design by a team of experts to identify any potential flaws or areas for improvement. Design reviews can be conducted at various stages of the design process, including the conceptual design phase, the detailed design phase, and the final design phase.

2. **Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (FMEA):** FMEA is a systematic approach used to identify and prioritize potential failures in a design. It involves analyzing the potential failure modes, their effects, and the likelihood of their occurrence. This information can then be used to prioritize design improvements and prevent potential failures.

3. **Design of Experiments (DOE):** DOE is a statistical method used to systematically vary the design parameters to determine the optimal design. This can help to identify the most critical design parameters and their optimal values, which can improve the quality of the design.

4. **Design for Six Sigma (DFSS):** DFSS is a data-driven approach to design that aims to achieve near-perfect quality. It involves using statistical methods to identify and eliminate potential defects in the design. This can help to reduce the number of defects in the final product or service.

5. **Lean Product Development:** Lean product development is a methodology that focuses on eliminating waste and reducing variation in the design process. This can help to improve the quality of the design by reducing the number of defects and improving the efficiency of the design process.

These quality control techniques can be used individually or in combination to ensure the quality of the design. They can help to identify and correct potential flaws in the design, leading to improved product or service quality.

#### 19.1c Case studies of quality in design

To further illustrate the importance of quality considerations in design, let's examine a few case studies.

##### Case Study 1: The Boeing 737 MAX Crisis

The Boeing 737 MAX crisis is a prime example of the consequences of poor quality control in design. The 737 MAX, a next-generation version of the popular 737 aircraft, was designed with a new automated flight control system. However, this system was not adequately tested or validated, leading to two fatal crashes in less than five months.

The crashes were caused by a design flaw in the automated flight control system, which could force the nose of the aircraft down in certain conditions. This flaw was not detected during the design process due to inadequate quality control measures. The crisis resulted in the grounding of all 737 MAX aircraft worldwide, significant financial losses for Boeing, and a tarnished reputation.

This case study highlights the importance of thorough quality control in design, including design reviews, failure mode and effects analysis, and design of experiments. It also underscores the need for continuous learning and improvement in the design process.

##### Case Study 2: The Apple Maps Fiasco

The Apple Maps fiasco is another example of the consequences of poor quality control in design. When Apple launched its Maps app in 2012, it replaced Google Maps as the default mapping service on iOS devices. However, the app was riddled with errors and omissions, leading to widespread user frustration and criticism.

The errors in the Maps app were due to a combination of factors, including inadequate testing, lack of quality control measures, and overconfidence in the design process. This case study underscores the importance of quality control in design, particularly in complex systems where errors can have significant consequences.

##### Case Study 3: The Tesla Autopilot Incident

The Tesla Autopilot incident is a recent example of the challenges of quality control in design for complex systems. In 2016, a Tesla Model S vehicle using the Autopilot system was involved in a fatal crash. The National Highway Traffic Safety Administration (NHTSA) found that the system was not designed to handle the specific scenario that led to the crash.

This case study highlights the challenges of quality control in design for complex systems, particularly those that rely on artificial intelligence and machine learning. It underscores the need for rigorous testing and validation, as well as ongoing learning and improvement in the design process.

In conclusion, these case studies underscore the importance of quality considerations in design. They highlight the need for thorough quality control measures, including design reviews, failure mode and effects analysis, design of experiments, and continuous learning and improvement. They also underscore the need for a systematic approach to design, as well as the importance of learning from past mistakes.




#### 19.1c Case studies in design for quality

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of quality considerations in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be used to improve the quality of designs.

##### Case Study 1: Design Review at Tesla

Tesla, a leading electric vehicle manufacturer, is known for its innovative design and quality control processes. One of the key techniques used by Tesla is design reviews. These reviews involve a thorough examination of the design by a team of experts, including engineers, designers, and quality control professionals. This process helps to identify potential flaws or areas for improvement in the design, which can then be addressed before the design is finalized.

In one instance, a design review at Tesla identified a potential issue with the battery pack of the Model S. The review team, which included experts from various disciplines, identified a potential failure mode in the battery pack that could lead to a fire. This issue was addressed immediately, and a redesign was implemented to prevent this potential failure. This case study highlights the importance of design reviews in identifying and addressing potential flaws in a design.

##### Case Study 2: Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (FMEA) at Boeing

Boeing, a leading aircraft manufacturer, uses FMEA as a key quality control technique. In the development of the 787 Dreamliner, Boeing conducted an FMEA on the aircraft's lithium-ion battery system. This analysis identified several potential failure modes, including overheating and short circuiting, which could lead to a fire.

Based on the FMEA, Boeing implemented several design changes, including the use of a different type of battery and the addition of a fire suppression system. These changes helped to prevent potential failures and improve the overall quality of the aircraft. This case study demonstrates the effectiveness of FMEA in identifying and addressing potential failures in a design.

##### Case Study 3: Design of Experiments (DOE) at Apple

Apple, a leading technology company, uses DOE as a key technique in its design process. In the development of the iPhone, Apple used DOE to optimize the design of the device's touch screen. By systematically varying the design parameters, Apple was able to identify the optimal design for the touch screen, which resulted in improved touch sensitivity and responsiveness.

This case study highlights the importance of DOE in optimizing design parameters and improving the quality of a product.

##### Case Study 4: Design for Six Sigma (DFSS) at Toyota

Toyota, a leading automobile manufacturer, uses DFSS as a key quality control technique. In the development of the Prius, Toyota used DFSS to reduce the number of defects in the vehicle's fuel cell system. By using statistical methods, Toyota was able to identify and eliminate potential defects, resulting in a near-perfect quality level for the fuel cell system.

This case study demonstrates the effectiveness of DFSS in reducing the number of defects in a design and improving the overall quality of a product.

##### Case Study 5: Lean Product Development at Zara

Zara, a leading fashion retailer, uses lean product development as a key technique in its design process. By eliminating waste and reducing variation in the design process, Zara is able to quickly respond to changing customer needs and market trends. This approach helps to improve the quality of the designs by reducing the number of defects and improving the efficiency of the design process.

This case study highlights the importance of lean product development in improving the quality of designs and responding to changing customer needs and market trends.




#### 19.2a Understanding Six Sigma

Six Sigma is a data-driven methodology that focuses on reducing defects and improving quality. It is based on the DMAIC (Define, Measure, Analyze, Improve, Control) process, which provides a structured approach to problem-solving and continuous improvement. Six Sigma aims to achieve near-perfect quality by identifying and eliminating sources of variation in the manufacturing process.

##### The Role of Six Sigma in Design

Six Sigma is not just a process improvement methodology; it is also a design philosophy. It is used in the design phase to ensure that products are designed to meet customer requirements and to minimize the likelihood of defects. This is achieved through the application of statistical tools and techniques to identify and eliminate potential sources of variation in the design.

##### Design for Six Sigma (DFSS)

Design for Six Sigma (DFSS) is a methodology that combines the principles of Six Sigma with design thinking. It is used to design products that meet customer requirements and are capable of delivering Six Sigma levels of performance. DFSS is particularly useful in the software industry, where the character and nature of software is different from other fields of engineering.

##### DFSS for Software

The application of DFSS in software is essentially a non-superficial modification of classical DFSS. The methodology describes the detailed process for successfully applying DFSS methods and tools throughout the software product design, covering the overall Software Development life cycle: requirements, architecture, design, implementation, integration, optimization, verification and validation (RADIOV).

The methodology explains how to build predictive statistical models for software reliability and robustness and shows how simulation and analysis techniques can be combined with structural design and architecture methods to effectively produce software and information systems at Six Sigma levels.

##### Blending Classical Modelling Techniques with Statistical, Predictive Models and Simulation Techniques

DFSS in software acts as a glue to blend the classical modelling techniques of software engineering such as object-oriented design or Evolutionary Rapid Development with statistical, predictive models and simulation techniques. This blending allows Software Engineers to measure and predict the quality attributes of the software product and also enables them to include software in system requirements and design.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the application of Six Sigma in design, exploring the various tools and techniques used in the DMAIC process.

#### 19.2b Implementing Six Sigma in Design

Implementing Six Sigma in design is a systematic process that involves the application of statistical tools and techniques to identify and eliminate potential sources of variation in the design. This process is guided by the DMAIC (Define, Measure, Analyze, Improve, Control) process, which provides a structured approach to problem-solving and continuous improvement.

##### Define

The Define phase is the first step in the DMAIC process. It involves defining the problem or opportunity, understanding the customer requirements, and setting the project goals. In the context of design, this phase is crucial as it helps to establish the design objectives and constraints. It also involves understanding the customer requirements and expectations, which are the basis for the design.

##### Measure

The Measure phase involves collecting data to understand the current state of the design. This data is used to establish a baseline against which the improvements will be measured. In the context of design, this phase involves measuring the performance of the design against the customer requirements and expectations. This is typically done through the use of design reviews and testing.

##### Analyze

The Analyze phase involves analyzing the data collected in the Measure phase to identify the root causes of any performance gaps. This is typically done through the use of statistical tools and techniques. In the context of design, this phase involves identifying the sources of variation in the design and developing solutions to eliminate these variations.

##### Improve

The Improve phase involves implementing the solutions developed in the Analyze phase to improve the design. This phase also involves verifying the effectiveness of the solutions through testing and validation.

##### Control

The Control phase involves establishing controls to ensure that the improvements made in the Improve phase are sustained over time. This involves monitoring the design performance and taking corrective actions as needed.

##### Design for Six Sigma (DFSS)

Design for Six Sigma (DFSS) is a methodology that combines the principles of Six Sigma with design thinking. It is used to design products that meet customer requirements and are capable of delivering Six Sigma levels of performance. DFSS is particularly useful in the software industry, where the character and nature of software is different from other fields of engineering.

##### DFSS for Software

The application of DFSS in software is essentially a non-superficial modification of classical DFSS. The methodology describes the detailed process for successfully applying DFSS methods and tools throughout the software product design, covering the overall Software Development life cycle: requirements, architecture, design, implementation, integration, optimization, verification and validation (RADIOV).

The methodology explains how to build predictive statistical models for software reliability and robustness and shows how simulation and analysis techniques can be combined with structural design and architecture methods to effectively produce software and information systems at Six Sigma levels.

##### Blending Classical Modelling Techniques with Statistical, Predictive Models and Simulation Techniques

DFSS in software acts as a glue to blend the classical modelling techniques of software engineering such as object-oriented design or Evolutionary Rapid Development with statistical, predictive models and simulation techniques. This blending allows Software Engineers to measure and predict the quality attributes of the software product and also enables them to include software in system requirements and design.

#### 19.2c Case studies in Six Sigma

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of Six Sigma in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the principles of Six Sigma can be applied to improve the quality and performance of designs.

##### Case Study 1: Improving the Performance of a Software System

In this case study, a software company was facing challenges with the performance of its system. The system was experiencing frequent crashes and performance degradation, leading to customer complaints and loss of revenue. The company decided to apply Six Sigma to address these issues.

The Define phase involved understanding the customer requirements and expectations, as well as the system performance requirements. The Measure phase involved collecting data on system performance, including crash logs and performance metrics. The Analyze phase involved analyzing the data to identify the root causes of the performance issues. This led to the development of solutions, including system optimizations and code refactoring, in the Improve phase. The Control phase involved implementing controls to monitor system performance and take corrective actions as needed.

As a result of these efforts, the system performance improved significantly, leading to a reduction in customer complaints and an increase in revenue.

##### Case Study 2: Designing a Robust Mechanical System

In this case study, a mechanical engineering company was designing a new product. The company decided to apply Six Sigma to ensure that the product met the required performance and quality standards.

The Define phase involved understanding the product requirements and constraints. The Measure phase involved collecting data on product performance, including test results and customer feedback. The Analyze phase involved analyzing the data to identify the root causes of any performance gaps. This led to the development of solutions, including design modifications and process improvements, in the Improve phase. The Control phase involved implementing controls to monitor product performance and take corrective actions as needed.

As a result of these efforts, the product met the required performance and quality standards, leading to successful product launch and increased customer satisfaction.

These case studies demonstrate the power of Six Sigma in improving the quality and performance of designs. By applying the principles of Six Sigma, companies can achieve significant improvements in their designs, leading to increased customer satisfaction and business success.




#### 19.2b Incorporating Six Sigma principles in design decisions

Incorporating Six Sigma principles in design decisions is a critical aspect of Design for Six Sigma (DFSS). This section will discuss how to apply Six Sigma principles in design decisions, with a focus on the DMAIC process and the role of statistical tools and techniques.

##### The DMAIC Process in Design

The DMAIC process is a structured problem-solving approach that is central to Six Sigma. It provides a systematic way to define problems, measure the current state, analyze the data, improve the process, and control the process to sustain the improvements. In the context of design, the DMAIC process can be used to ensure that products are designed to meet customer requirements and to minimize the likelihood of defects.

##### Define

In the Define phase, the design team defines the problem or opportunity. This involves understanding the customer requirements, product specifications, and design constraints. The team also identifies the design objectives and metrics that will be used to measure the success of the design.

##### Measure

In the Measure phase, the team collects data to understand the current state of the design. This involves measuring the performance of the design against the defined metrics and identifying any gaps or areas of concern. Statistical tools and techniques can be used to analyze the data and identify potential sources of variation in the design.

##### Analyze

In the Analyze phase, the team uses statistical analysis to identify the root causes of any problems or gaps in the design. This involves using tools such as root cause analysis, failure mode and effects analysis, and design of experiments to understand the underlying causes of any issues.

##### Improve

In the Improve phase, the team implements solutions to address the root causes identified in the Analyze phase. This may involve redesigning parts of the product, changing the manufacturing process, or implementing new quality control measures. The team uses statistical tools and techniques to measure the effectiveness of the improvements and make any necessary adjustments.

##### Control

In the Control phase, the team implements controls to sustain the improvements made in the Improve phase. This involves monitoring the design performance and implementing corrective actions as needed. Statistical process control can be used to detect any changes in the design performance and take corrective action before they lead to defects or quality issues.

By incorporating Six Sigma principles in the design process, engineers can ensure that products are designed to meet customer requirements and are capable of delivering Six Sigma levels of performance. This not only improves the quality of the products but also reduces the likelihood of defects and rework, leading to significant cost savings.

#### 19.2c Case studies of Six Sigma in design

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of Six Sigma principles in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the DMAIC process and statistical tools and techniques can be used to improve product quality and reduce defects.

##### Case Study 1: Reducing Defects in a Manufacturing Process

A manufacturing company was experiencing high defect rates in their production process. The company decided to apply Six Sigma principles to address the issue. The DMAIC process was used to define the problem, measure the current state, analyze the data, improve the process, and control the process.

In the Define phase, the team identified the customer requirements and product specifications. They also defined the design objectives and metrics that would be used to measure the success of the design.

In the Measure phase, the team collected data on the current state of the process. This involved measuring the performance of the process against the defined metrics and identifying any gaps or areas of concern. Statistical tools and techniques were used to analyze the data and identify potential sources of variation in the process.

In the Analyze phase, the team used statistical analysis to identify the root causes of the high defect rates. This involved using tools such as root cause analysis, failure mode and effects analysis, and design of experiments to understand the underlying causes of the defects.

In the Improve phase, the team implemented solutions to address the root causes of the defects. This involved redesigning parts of the process, changing the manufacturing process, and implementing new quality control measures. The team used statistical tools and techniques to measure the effectiveness of the improvements and make any necessary adjustments.

In the Control phase, the team implemented controls to sustain the improvements made in the Improve phase. This involved monitoring the process performance and implementing corrective actions as needed.

As a result of this project, the company was able to reduce the defect rate by 80%, leading to significant cost savings and improved customer satisfaction.

##### Case Study 2: Improving the Performance of a Software System

A software company was experiencing performance issues with their system. The company decided to apply Six Sigma principles to address the issue. The DMAIC process was used to define the problem, measure the current state, analyze the data, improve the process, and control the process.

In the Define phase, the team identified the customer requirements and system specifications. They also defined the design objectives and metrics that would be used to measure the success of the design.

In the Measure phase, the team collected data on the current state of the system. This involved measuring the performance of the system against the defined metrics and identifying any gaps or areas of concern. Statistical tools and techniques were used to analyze the data and identify potential sources of variation in the system.

In the Analyze phase, the team used statistical analysis to identify the root causes of the performance issues. This involved using tools such as root cause analysis, failure mode and effects analysis, and design of experiments to understand the underlying causes of the performance issues.

In the Improve phase, the team implemented solutions to address the root causes of the performance issues. This involved redesigning parts of the system, changing the system architecture, and implementing new performance optimization measures. The team used statistical tools and techniques to measure the effectiveness of the improvements and make any necessary adjustments.

In the Control phase, the team implemented controls to sustain the improvements made in the Improve phase. This involved monitoring the system performance and implementing corrective actions as needed.

As a result of this project, the company was able to improve the system performance by 50%, leading to improved customer satisfaction and reduced support costs.




#### 19.2c Case studies in Six Sigma in design

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of Six Sigma principles in design. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the DMAIC process and statistical tools and techniques are used in the design process.

##### Case Study 1: Reducing Defects in a Software Product

In this case study, a software company was facing high defect rates in their product, leading to customer complaints and increased support costs. The company decided to apply Six Sigma principles to address this issue.

###### Define

The design team defined the problem as high defect rates and identified the design objectives as reducing defects and improving customer satisfaction.

###### Measure

The team collected data on the number of defects, the types of defects, and the customer impact of these defects. They also measured customer satisfaction levels.

###### Analyze

Statistical analysis revealed that the majority of defects were caused by a few key issues, such as inadequate testing and lack of design reviews.

###### Improve

The team implemented several solutions, including improved testing procedures, design reviews, and customer feedback mechanisms.

###### Control

The team established control measures, including regular reviews of defect rates and customer satisfaction levels, to ensure that the improvements were sustained.

##### Case Study 2: Improving the Reliability of a Mechanical Component

In this case study, a manufacturing company was experiencing high failure rates in a critical mechanical component. The company decided to apply Six Sigma principles to address this issue.

###### Define

The design team defined the problem as high failure rates and identified the design objectives as improving reliability and reducing costs.

###### Measure

The team collected data on the failure rates, the causes of failures, and the costs associated with failures.

###### Analyze

Statistical analysis revealed that the majority of failures were caused by a few key issues, such as material defects and design flaws.

###### Improve

The team implemented several solutions, including improved material selection, design modifications, and enhanced manufacturing processes.

###### Control

The team established control measures, including regular monitoring of failure rates and costs, to ensure that the improvements were sustained.

These case studies demonstrate the power of Six Sigma principles in improving product quality. By applying the DMAIC process and statistical tools and techniques, design teams can identify and address the root causes of problems, leading to significant improvements in product quality.




#### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of design for quality in engineering. We have discussed the importance of considering quality in the design process and how it can impact the overall success of a product. We have also examined various techniques and tools that can be used to ensure quality in the design, such as Design of Experiments (DOE) and Failure Modes and Effects Analysis (FMEA).

One key takeaway from this chapter is the importance of understanding the relationship between design and quality. By incorporating quality considerations into the design process, engineers can prevent costly redesigns and ensure that the final product meets the desired performance and reliability standards. This not only saves time and resources, but also helps to build trust and confidence in the product among customers.

Another important aspect of design for quality is the role of prototyping. By creating prototypes and conducting testing and evaluation, engineers can identify and address potential quality issues early on in the design process. This allows for continuous improvement and refinement of the design, leading to a higher quality final product.

In conclusion, design for quality is a crucial aspect of engineering design and rapid prototyping. By considering quality in the design process and utilizing techniques such as DOE and FMEA, engineers can ensure that their products meet the desired quality standards and provide value to their customers.
